synaptic **AMPA receptor (AMPAR) phenotype** in cerebellar stellate cells.\n- This switch involves increased **GluR2 mRNA** and synaptic incorporation of **GluR2-containing, Ca$^{2+}$-impermeable Fear-induced switch in synaptic AMPAR phenotype\n- A **single fear-inducing stimulus** triggers a transcription-dependent switch in synaptic **AMPAR phenotype** in cerebellar stellate cells, changing from GluR2-lacking (Ca$^{2+}$-permeable) to GluR2-containing (Ca$^{2+}$-impermeable) receptors within hours.\n- This switch is driven by **noradrenaline**, which prolongs action potentials, increases Ca$^{2+}$ entry, and activates the **ERK/MAPK pathway** to boost *GluR2* gene transcription.\n- The result is a long-lasting change in synaptic function, potentially altering the activity of inhibitory neural networks in response to fear.\n\n### üìå Role of noradrenaline and Œ≤-adrenergic receptors\n- **Noradrenaline** released during emotional arousal activates **Œ≤-adrenergic receptors** in the brain, especially in cerebellar stellate cells.\n- Activation of Œ≤-adrenergic receptors increases action potential duration and intracellular $Ca^{2+}$, triggering ERK/MAPK signaling and transcription of GluR2, leading to a switch in synaptic **AMPAR phenotype**.\n- This process enables a long-lasting change in synaptic strength and may underlie how emotional experiences modify learning and memory.\n\n### üìå Mechanism: Action potential broadening, Ca2+ entry, ERK/MAPK signaling, and GluR2 transcription\n- **Action potential broadening** increases **Ca$^{2+}$ entry** through L-type channels, activating the **ERK/MAPK signaling** pathway.\n- This cascade triggers **GluR2 gene transcription**, leading to more **GluR2-containing AMPARs** at synapses and a switch in receptor phenotype.\n\n### üìå Transcription-dependent vs. independent synaptic plasticity\n- **Transcription-dependent synaptic plasticity** involves long-lasting changes in synaptic strength that require new gene transcription, such as the noradrenaline-triggered switch to GluR2-containing AMPARs after fear stimuli.\n- **Transcription-independent synaptic plasticity** relies on rapid mechanisms like receptor trafficking or local protein synthesis, enabling fast changes in synaptic function without altering gene expression.\n- **Key difference:** Transcription-dependent changes occur over hours and affect the whole neuron, while transcription-independent changes happen within minutes and are often synapse-specific.\n\n### üìå Functional consequences for inhibitory neural circuits\n- **Inhibitory neural circuits** can undergo long-lasting changes in synaptic strength when a single fear-inducing stimulus triggers transcription-dependent upregulation of GluR2, leading to a switch in AMPAR phenotype.\n- This switch reduces **Ca$^{2+}$ permeability** at synapses, altering **synaptic efficacy** and potentially reshaping the activity of the entire inhibitory network.\n- Application of TEA (1 mM with kynurenic acid and picrotoxin, 3 h) altered the I-V relation of the sEPSC from inwardly rectifying to nearly linear and increased the rectification index (control, 0.29 ¬± 0.03, n = 5; TEA, 0.88 ¬± 0.09, n = 6; P < 0.005; Fig. 4b,c), indicative of a switch from GluR2-lacking to GluR2-containing AMPARs. Although TEA increased the sEPSC amplitude at +40 mV (control, 7.9 ¬± 0.5; TEA, 18.5 ¬± 1.6 pA; n = 6, P < 0.005), there was little or no change at ‚àí60 mV. However, TEA increased the decay time of sEPSCs at ‚àí60 mV (Fig. 4d). These results suggest that TEA promotes synaptic incorpo¬≠ ration of GluR2-containing receptors. Although TEA treatment for 1 h did not alter the sEPSC rectification index (0.31 ¬± 0.03, n = 3; Fig. 4c), TEA treatment for 1 h, followed by a 2-h incubation in control solution, significantly increased the rectification index of synaptic cur¬≠ rents (0.60 ¬± 0.09, n = 3, P < 0.05 versus control). Such experience-dependent neuronal plasticity can be long-lasting and is thought to involve the regulation of gene transcription. We found that a single fear-inducing stimulus increased GluR2 (also known as Gria2) mRNA abundance and promoted synaptic incorporation of GluR2-containing AMPA receptors (AMPARs) in mouse cerebellar stellate cells. The switch in synaptic AMPAR phenotype was mediated by noradrenaline and action potential prolongation. The subsequent rise in intracellular Ca2+ and activation of Ca2+-sensitive ERK/MAPK signaling triggered new GluR2 gene transcription and a switch in the synaptic AMPAR phenotype from GluR2-lacking, Ca2+-permeable receptors to GluR2-containing, Ca2+-impermeable receptors on the order of hours. Such experience-dependent neuronal plasticity can be long-lasting and is thought to involve the regulation of gene transcription. We found that a single fear-inducing stimulus increased GluR2 (also known as Gria2) mRNA abundance and promoted synaptic incorporation of GluR2-containing AMPA receptors (AMPARs) in mouse cerebellar stellate cells. The switch in synaptic AMPAR phenotype was mediated by noradrenaline and action potential prolongation. <li><strong>Transcription-dependent synaptic plasticity</strong> involves long-lasting changes in synaptic strength that require new gene transcription, such as the noradrenaline-triggered switch to GluR2-containing AMPARs after fear stimuli.</li> <li><strong>Transcription-independent synaptic plasticity</strong> relies on rapid mechanisms like receptor trafficking or local protein synthesis, mRNA</strong> and synaptic incorporation of <strong>GluR2-containing, Ca<sup>2+</sup>-impermeable AMPARs</strong>, replacing GluR2-lacking, Ca<sup>2+</sup>-permeable receptors.</li> <li>The process is mediated by <strong>noradrenaline</strong>, which prolongs action potentials, enhances Ca<sup>2+</sup> entry, and activates <strong>ERK/MAPK signaling</strong> to drive new GluR2 gene transcription.</li> <li>This <strong>phenotype switch</strong> alters synaptic efficacy and may reshape inhibitory neural network activity in response to emotional stress.</li> </ul> <h3>üìå Fear-induced switch in synaptic AMPAR phenotype</h3> <ul> <li>A <strong>single fear-inducing stimulus</strong> triggers a transcription-dependent switch in synaptic <strong>AMPAR phenotype</strong> in cerebellar stellate cells, changing from GluR2-lacking (Ca<sup>2+</sup>-permeable) to GluR2-containing (Ca<sup>2+</sup>-impermeable) receptors within hours.</li> <li>This switch is driven by <strong>noradrenaline</strong>, which prolongs action potentials, increases Ca<sup>2+</sup> entry, and activates the <strong>ERK/MAPK pathway</strong> to boost <em>GluR2</em> gene transcription.</li> <li>The result is a long-lasting change in synaptic function, potentially altering the activity of inhibitory neural networks in response to fear.</li> </ul> <h3>üìå Role of noradrenaline and Œ≤-adrenergic receptors</h3> <ul> <li><strong>Noradrenaline</strong> released during emotional arousal activates <strong>Œ≤-adrenergic receptors</strong> in the brain, especially in cerebellar stellate cells.</li> <li>Activation of Œ≤-adrenergic receptors increases action potential duration and intracellular $Ca^{2+}$, triggering ERK/MAPK signaling and transcription of GluR2, leading to a switch in synaptic <strong>AMPAR phenotype</strong>.</li> <li>This process enables a long-lasting change in synaptic strength and may underlie how emotional experiences modify learning and memory.</li> </ul> <h3>üìå Mechanism: Action potential broadening, Ca2+ entry, ERK/MAPK signaling, and GluR2 transcription</h3> <ul> <li><strong>Action potential broadening</strong> increases <strong>Ca<sup>2+</sup> entry</strong> through L-type channels, activating the <strong>ERK/MAPK signaling</strong> pathway.</li> <li>This cascade triggers <strong>GluR2 gene transcription</strong>, leading to more <strong>GluR2-containing AMPARs</strong> at synapses and a switch in receptor phenotype.</li> </ul> <h3>üìå Transcription-dependent vs. independent synaptic plasticity</h3> <ul> <li><strong>Transcription-dependent synaptic plasticity</strong> involves long-lasting changes in synaptic strength that require new gene transcription, such as the noradrenaline-triggered switch to GluR2-containing AMPARs after fear stimuli.</li> <li><strong>Transcription-independent synaptic plasticity</strong> relies on rapid mechanisms like receptor trafficking or local protein synthesis, enabling fast changes in synaptic function without altering gene expression.</li> <li><strong>Key difference:</strong> Transcription-dependent changes occur over hours and affect the whole neuron, while transcription-independent changes happen within minutes and are often synapse-specific.</li> </ul> <h3>üìå Functional consequences for inhibitory neural circuits</h3> <ul> <li><strong>Inhibitory neural circuits</strong> can undergo long-lasting changes in synaptic strength when a single fear-inducing stimulus triggers transcription-dependent upregulation of GluR2, leading to a switch in AMPAR phenotype.</li> <li>This switch reduces <strong>Ca<sup>2+</sup> permeability</strong> at synapses, altering <strong>synaptic efficacy</strong> and potentially reshaping the activity of the entire inhibitory network.</li> <li><strong>Noradrenaline</strong> and <strong>action potential broadening</strong> are key signals that activate the <strong>ERK/MAPK pathway</strong>, driving these transcriptional and functional changes.</li> </ul> <hr/> <h3>üí¨ Ask Me Anything!</h3> <p>Feel free to ask me any questions about the document!
